Why Specific Knowledge and Ignorance Cannot Be Created
Second case of the third proof: impossibility of creating specific knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating specific knowledge or ignorance leads to a vicious circle, proving that only Allah can create these mental states.

Core Idea
To intend specific knowledge, you must already know the object — an impossible circularity.

The second part of the proof tackles the attempt to bring about knowledge of a particular thing. The reasoning reveals an inescapable regress: to intend knowledge, one must distinguish it from ignorance, which requires prior knowledge of the object itself. The same logic applies to ignorance, showing that neither can be self-induced.

Guided Path
1
Read the second case passage
Read from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to the end. Notice the distinction between knowledge (ʿilm) and ignorance (jahl) based on correspondence (muṭābaqa).
Correspondence of belief to reality defines knowledge; lack of it defines ignorance.
2
Follow the circularity for knowledge
To intend knowledge of something, one must first know that the belief corresponds, which requires knowing the object itself beforehand. Thus one would need to already know the object to intend to know it — a vicious circle.
This shows the impossibility (muḥāl) of creating specific knowledge.
3
Apply the same to ignorance
Similarly, to intend ignorance, one must know the non-correspondence, which again requires prior knowledge of the object. This makes both creation of knowledge and ignorance impossible for the servant.
The conclusion: all knowledges and ignorances are from Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث على أن العبد لا يقدر على خلق العلـوم هو : أن العبد إذا حاول إحداث العلم، إما ابتداء وإما بواسطة شيء آخر . فإما أن يحاول إحداث مطلق العلم ، وإما أن يحاول إحداث العلم بكذا على التعيين . فإن حاول إحداث مطلق العلم ، لم يكن بأن يحصل هذا العلم ، أولى بأن يحصل ذلك العلم . لأن ماهية العلم جنس تحته أنواع كثيرة . وهي العلم بهذا المعلوم، والعلم بهذا المعلوم الآخر . والطبيعة الجنسية بالنسبة إلى جميع الأنواع على السوية . فلم تكن الطبيعة الجنسية [ ناقصة (٢) ] باقتضاء بعض الأنواع، أولى من اقتضاء النوع الآخر. فلو كان القصد إلى جنس حصول النوع ، كان ذلك رجحاناً للممكن من غير مرجح . وهو محال . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : إنه يحاول إحداث العلم بكذا على التعيين . فنقول : هذا أيضاً محال . وذلك لأن اعتقاد أن الشيء كذا ، قد يكون علماً - إذا كان مطابقاً لذلك المعتقد وقد يكون جهلاً ، وهو إذا كان غير مطابق . فالعلم إنما يتميز عن الجهل ، إذا عرف كونه مطابقاً لذلك المعتقد 1 وقد يكون جهلا ، وهو إذا كان غير مطابق (۱) ] فعلى هذا لا يمكن أن يقصد إلى العلم دون الجهل ، إلا إذا ميز بين العلم وبين الجهل ، ولا يمكنه أن يعرف هذا الامتياز إلا إذا عرف أن هذا الاعتقاد مطابق للمعتقد ، ولا يمكنه أن يعرف هذه المطابقة ، إلا إذا عرف أو لا حال ذلك المعتقد في نفسه . فثبت : أنه لا يمكن أن يجعل نفسه عالماً بذلك الشيء ، إلا إذا كان قد عرف أولاً حال ذلك الشيء . وذلك يقتضي كون الشيء مشروطاً بنفسه . وهو محال . فثبت : أن إقدار الانسان على جعل نفسه ، عالماً بشيء : أمر محال . وبهذا البرهان يظهر أيضاً : أن الإنسان لا يمكنه أن يجعل نفسه جاهلاً بشيء . لأن الجهل إنما يتميز عن العلم ، بكونه غير مطابق للمعتقد. ولا يمكنه أن يعرف كونه غير مطابق للمعتقد ، إلا إذا عرف قبل ذلك ، حال ذلك المعتقد في نفسه . فثبت : أنه لا يمكنه تحصيل الجهل لنفسه ، إلا إذا كان عالماً بذلك الشيء . وهذا يقتضي كون أحد الضدين مشروطاً بالآخر ، وإنه محال . فثبت بهذه البراهين القاطعة : أن كل ما حصل في قلوب الخلق وعقولهم من العلوم والجهلات ، فالكل من الله [ وبإيجاد الله (۲) ] .
The third proof that the servant cannot create knowledge is: That when the servant attempts to bring about knowledge, either initially or through something else. Then either he attempts to bring about absolute knowledge (muṭlaq al-ʿilm), or he attempts to bring about knowledge of something specific (al-ʿilm bi-kadhā ʿalā al-taʿyīn). If he attempts to bring about absolute knowledge, it is not more likely that this knowledge obtains rather than that knowledge. Because the essence (māhiyya) of knowledge is a genus (jins) under which there are many types. They are knowledge of this known thing, and knowledge of that other known thing. And the generic nature in relation to all types is equal. So the generic nature [deficient (2)?] in requiring some types is not more likely than requiring the other type. So if the intention were toward the genus of obtaining a type, that would be a preponderance of a possible without a preponderator (murajjiḥ). That is impossible. As for the second division, which is that it is said: He attempts to bring about knowledge of something specifically. We say: This is also impossible. That is because the belief that something is so-and-so may be knowledge if it corresponds to that believed thing, and may be ignorance (jahl) if it does not correspond. So knowledge is only distinguished from ignorance if he knows that it corresponds to that believed thing. [And it may be ignorance if it does not correspond (1)] So on this basis, he cannot intend knowledge without ignorance unless he distinguishes between knowledge and ignorance, and he cannot know this distinction unless he knows that this belief corresponds to the believed thing, and he cannot know this correspondence unless he first knows the state of that believed thing in itself. So it is established that he cannot make himself knowledgeable of that thing unless he has first known the state of that thing. That requires the thing to be conditioned by itself, which is impossible. So it is established that enabling man to make himself knowledgeable of something is an impossible matter. And by this proof it also becomes clear that man cannot make himself ignorant of something. Because ignorance is only distinguished from knowledge by being non-corresponding to the believed thing. And he cannot know that it is non-corresponding unless he previously knows the state of that believed thing in itself. So it is established that he cannot obtain ignorance for himself unless he is knowledgeable of that thing. That requires one of the two opposites to be conditioned by the other, and that is impossible. So it is established by these conclusive proofs that whatever occurs in the hearts and minds of creatures, of knowledges and ignorances, all of it is from Allah [and by Allah's bringing into existence (2)].
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that the servant cannot create knowledge. The proof is divided into two cases. First, if one attempts to bring about absolute knowledge, the generic nature of knowledge cannot favor one specific knowledge over another without a preponderator, which is impossible. Second, if one attempts to bring about knowledge of something specific, a circularity arises: to intend knowledge, one must first distinguish it from ignorance by knowing its correspondence to the object, which requires prior knowledge of the object. Thus, one would need to know the object before knowing it, an impossibility. The same reasoning applies to ignorance, requiring prior knowledge to recognize non-correspondence. The author concludes that all knowledges and ignorances are created by Allah alone.

Key Terms
علم
جهل
اعتقاد
مطابقة
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can simply intend to acquire knowledge of something by concentrating or willing it.
Correction: The proof shows that intending knowledge already presupposes knowledge of the object, so one cannot initiate knowledge through intention alone.

Checkpoint
According to the second part of the third proof, why cannot a person intend to become ignorant of something?
A) Ignorance is not a real mental state
B) To intend ignorance, one must recognize non-correspondence, which requires prior knowledge of the object
C) Ignorance is the same as knowledge
D) The person lacks the power to choose

Key Takeaway
Creating specific knowledge or ignorance is impossible because both require prior knowledge of the object, leading to a vicious circle; thus all such states come from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot make yourself knowledgeable of a single thing without prior knowledge from Allah, when you sit to learn, do you realize that every piece of understanding you gain is a direct gift from Him? How would your attitude during study change if you truly believed that?
?
The proof shows that even intending ignorance requires knowledge of what you are avoiding. Think of something you have deliberately ignored in your faith or life. Were you not, in that very moment, aware of what you were turning away from? What does that say about your accountability?
?
All knowledge and ignorance in your heart is from Allah alone. If He is the source, are you using the knowledge He has given you for His sake? And are you content with the ignorance you harbor, knowing He could have granted you understanding but did not? What will you do today to seek beneficial knowledge with humility and gratitude?

Objection and First Answer: The Chain of Ignorances Ends
Objection and first answer: chain of ignorances ends in first ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge might be produced via prior sciences and the first answer which asserts that the chain of ignorances ends in a first ignorance created by Allah.

Core Idea
The chain of ignorances cannot be infinite; it ends in a first ignorance from Allah.

This passage presents a crucial objection against the Ash'ari position that only Allah creates knowledge and ignorance. The objector suggests that even if one cannot create knowledge directly, perhaps one can produce it through a chain of prior knowledges or ignorances. The author responds by showing that such a chain cannot regress infinitely — it must terminate in a first ignorance, which is created by Allah, not by the servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting how the objector frames the possibility of producing knowledge/ignorance via prior sciences. Then read the translation.
Notice the phrase 'بواسطة علوم متقدمة عليها' — 'through prior knowledges'.
2
Identify the first answer
The answer has two aspects; this passage gives the first. Find the key statement: 'تلك الجهالات لا تتسلسل، بل تنتهي إلى جهل أول' — 'Those ignorances do not form an infinite chain, but end in a first ignorance.'
The second aspect is not given here but is mentioned.
3
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
Reflect on why an infinite chain of prior ignorances is rationally impossible. The author asserts it ends, implying a created first ignorance.
This connects to the broader theological point that only Allah creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وإن كان لا يمكنه إحداث العلوم والجهالات في نفسه ابتداء ، إلا أنه يمكنه إحداثها بواسطة علوم متقدمة عليها ، أو جهالات متقدمة عليها ؟ قلنا : الجواب من وجهين : الأول : إن تلك الجهالات لا تتسلسل ، بل تنتهي إلى جهل أول . فكون خالقه هو الله سبحانه
If they say: Why is it not permissible to say: Even if he cannot bring about knowledges and ignorances in himself initially, he can bring them about through prior knowledges or prior ignorances? We say: The answer is from two aspects: The first: Those ignorances do not form an infinite chain, but rather end in a first ignorance. So its Creator is Allah, Exalted.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the first part of the answer. The objector suggests that even if one cannot create knowledge or ignorance directly, one might produce them through prior knowledges or ignorances, i.e., by means of a chain. The author responds that such a chain cannot regress infinitely; it must terminate in a first ignorance. This first ignorance is created by Allah, not by the servant. Thus, the ultimate source of all cognition remains divine.

Key Terms
جهل
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite regress of prior ignorances is possible.
Correction: The author argues that such a chain must terminate in a first ignorance, which is created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what does the chain of ignorances end in?
A) An infinite regress
B) A first ignorance
C) A first knowledge
D) The servant's choice

Key Takeaway
The chain of prior ignorances cannot regress infinitely; it ends in a first ignorance that is created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every moment of confusion or doubt in your life ultimately traces back to a first ignorance created by Allah. Does that thought make you more patient with your own lack of knowledge, or does it create a sense of urgency to seek the truth?
?
The objector wanted to distance Allah from human ignorance, but the answer affirms that even the first ignorance is from Allah. How does this shape your view of personal responsibility for what you don't know? Are you quick to blame yourself or others, or do you acknowledge Allah’s wisdom behind your limitations?
?
If the chain of questions about why you are ignorant cannot go on forever, what is your 'first ignorance' that you must simply accept as part of Allah's decree? Can you submit to His ultimate knowledge without needing every answer?

The Jabariyya's Arbitrary Specification of Faith and Unbelief
The author presents the third objection: jabariyya leads to arbitrary specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third aspect of the critique against Jabariyya: that their denial of secondary causes leads to an arbitrary distinction between believers and unbelievers.

Core Idea
If only God creates actions, then faith and disbelief become arbitrary.

The author presents a third objection against the Jabariyya. If we accept that only God is the cause of all contingent things, then the difference between a believer and an unbeliever has no explanation—it is a mere arbitrary specification. This passage highlights a logical problem within the Jabariyya framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's argument come through before you analyze it.
Notice the structure: premise, observation, conclusion.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify the central claim of the Jabariyya: 'no efficient cause in the origination of contingent things except God.' Understand why this is the starting point.
This is the premise from which the consequence follows.
3
Understand the consequence
The author argues that this leads to a specification without a specifier. Grasp why an arbitrary assignment of faith and disbelief is problematic for a rational theological system.
The term 'specification without a specifier' (تخصيص لا لمخصص) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن مذهب الجبرية : أنه لا مؤثر في حدوث الممكنات إلا الله، وإذا كان كذلك، كان تخصيص بعض الناس بخلق الكفر والمعصية فيه، والبعض الآخر بخلق الإيمان والطاعة . تخصيصاً لا لمخصص
The third aspect: The doctrine of the Jabariyya is that there is no efficient cause in the origination of contingent things except God. And if it is so, then specifying some people with the creation of disbelief and disobedience in them, and others with the creation of faith and obedience, is a specification without a specifier.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The jabariyya claim that only God causes the coming into existence of contingent things. Therefore, when we see that God creates disbelief and sin in some people and faith and obedience in others, this differentiation is arbitrary—there is no reason or cause that explains why one person gets faith and another gets disbelief. The author calls this a 'specification without a specifier' (takhsees laa li mukhasis), implying it is irrational.

Key Terms
مؤثر
الممكنات
تخصيص
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying that God can create differently in different people.
Correction: The author is not denying God's power; he is pointing out that the Jabariyya view leads to an arbitrary distinction, which is rationally problematic.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author see in the jabariyya view that only God creates actions?
A) It denies human free will.
B) It makes God the author of evil.
C) It makes the distinction between believers and unbelievers arbitrary.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Jabariyya position leads to an arbitrary assignment of faith and disbelief, which the author deems irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your own faith is arbitrary—that you could have been born into another religion or circumstance? How does this passage's argument against arbitrary specification affect your confidence that your faith is not a random assignment but has a reason behind it? Will you thank God tonight for giving you faith, not as something deserved, but as a gift with purpose?
?
When you see someone who does not believe, do you ever assume it is simply because God did not give them faith? Does this lesson challenge you to think differently about the relationship between divine decree and human responsibility? How will you adjust your attitude toward non-believers knowing that the specification of faith cannot be arbitrary in the sight of the author?

The Motive Dilemma Against Mu'tazila
A dilemma argument against Mu'tazili views on motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazili view on motive either leads to determinism or undermines the evidential value of miracles.

Core Idea
If action depends on motive, determinism follows; if not, miracles lose their proof.

This passage presents a powerful dilemma aimed at the Mu'tazila. The author argues that their stance on the role of motive in human action forces them into a choice between two unacceptable results: either determinism (which they deny) or the collapse of miracle-based proof for prophethood. Understanding this dilemma is key to grasping the Ash'ari critique of Mu'tazili causality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma's setup
The passage begins by presenting two options for the Mu'tazila: either action depends on motive (da'i) or it does not. This framing sets up the entire argument.
Notice that the dilemma is exhaustive.
2
Examine the first horn: determinism
If action depends on motive, then absence of motive makes action impossible, and presence makes it necessary. This is determinism (jabr), which the Mu'tazila reject. The author points out that this is exactly what they accuse the Ash'aris of.
The argument turns the tables on the Mu'tazila.
3
Examine the second horn: miracles fail
If action does not depend on motive, then God could perform a miracle without any purpose, so the miracle cannot prove the prophet's truthfulness. This undermines a core proof for prophethood.
The Mu'tazila rely on miracles as evidence, so this is a serious problem.
4
Reflect on the force of the dilemma
Both horns lead to consequences the Mu'tazila reject. The passage shows their position is self-contradictory or leads to unacceptable outcomes.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن المعتزلة . إما أن يحكموا بأن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي ، أو لا يحكموا بذلك. فإن كان الأول لزمهم الجبر . وذلك لأن عند عدم الداعي يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه ، وعند وجوده يجب صدور الفعل عنه . وتكون أفعال العباد معلولات أفعال الله تعالى . وحينئذ يلزمهم ما ألزموه علينا . وإن كان الثاني . وهو أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعي ، فعلى هذا التقدير يخرج المعجز عن كونه دليلا على الصدق . لاحتمال : أنه تعالى خلق ذلك المعجز . لا لداعية ولا لغرض أصلاً . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإنه يخرج المعجز عن كونه دليلاً على صدق المدعي .
The second: The Mu'tazila either rule that the action depends on the motive (da'i), or they do not rule that. If the first, then determinism (jabr) follows for them. That is because when the motive is absent, the action is impossible to occur from him, and when it is present, the action necessarily occurs from him. And the actions of servants become effects of the actions of God. And then what they required of us becomes required of them. And if the second—namely that the occurrence of the action from the capable agent does not depend on the motive—then on this assumption the miracle (mu'jiz) ceases to be a proof of truthfulness. Because of the possibility that God created that miracle without any motive or purpose at all. And on this assumption, the miracle ceases to be a proof of the claimant's truthfulness.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the second rebuttal against the Mu'tazila. The author poses a dilemma regarding the role of motive (da'i) in human action. The Mu'tazila have two options. First option: they hold that action depends on a motive. If so, then when the motive is absent, the action is impossible; when the motive is present, the action is necessitated. This makes human actions effects (ma'lulat) of God's actions, which is essentially determinism (jabr). This is exactly what the Mu'tazila accuse the Ash'aris of, so the objection turns back on them. Second option: they hold that action does not depend on a motive. In that case, a miracle (mu'jiz) performed by God at the hand of a prophet could occur without any motive or purpose. That would mean the miracle is not necessarily a sign of truthfulness because it could happen for no reason. Thus, the miracle loses its probative value. Either way, the Mu'tazila face a problem. So the second rebuttal shows that the Mu'tazili position is self-contradictory or leads to consequences they reject.

Key Terms
الداعي (motive/incentive)
الجبر (determinism)
القادر (capable agent)
المعجز (miracle)
المدعي (claimant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that only Ash'aris are determinists, but this passage shows that if action depends on motive, even Mu'tazila fall into determinism.
Correction: The dilemma demonstrates that the Mu'tazili position on motive either commits them to determinism or undermines their own proof for prophethood (miracles).

Checkpoint
If the Mu'tazila hold that action depends on a motive (da'i), what follows according to this passage?
A) Actions become free and independent.
B) Determinism (jabr) follows, and actions become effects of God's actions.
C) Miracles become impossible.
D) The servant becomes the sole creator of his actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili view on motive leads to a dilemma: either determinism or loss of miracle's evidential value, both of which they reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever reflect on whether it was truly your choice or if Allah created the motive in you? How does this dilemma push you to reconsider your own sense of agency?
?
If a miracle could occur without any divine purpose, how would that affect your trust in the proofs of prophethood? Examine your heart: do you rely on such signs, or do you look for deeper certainty?
?
The Mu'tazila were forced into a contradiction because they refused to accept divine control over all actions. Are there areas in your life where you resist Allah's complete authority, insisting on your own independence? What would it take for you to submit fully to His will?

Why the Jabriyya Cannot Use the Qur'an as Proof
Author argues that Jabriyya view undermines the Qur'an as a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Jabriyya theological position, which denies human agency, fundamentally prevents them from relying on the Qur'an as a proof text due to the collapse of the literal presumption in speech.

Core Idea
If all deception is from God, the literal presumption collapses.

This passage presents a critical argument against the Jabriyya: their own determinism robs them of the very tool they need to argue from the Qur'an. By attributing all actions, including deception, to God, they cannot claim that non-literal speech without indication would imply a deceptive speaker. The author shows that the standard justifications for the literal presumption fail on their own premises.

Guided Path
1
The literal presumption
Understand the key premise: the default in speech is literal meaning (al-haqiqah). This is foundational for using any revealed text as evidence.
2
Its standard justification
Learn the two usual arguments for this presumption: non-literal speech without indication would imply ignorance or deception, or it would impose an unbearable obligation.
3
The Jabriyya objection
See why a determinist who believes all actions, including deception, are created by God cannot accept these justifications. They would have to say God is the source of confusion, and the unbearable obligation claim backfires because God creates disbelief and then commands faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان أن القول بأن العبد غير موجد لأفعال نفسه ، يمنع من الاعتراف بأن القرآن حجة . لو أن مدار التمسك بالقرآن ، وبسائر الدلائل اللفظية على أصل واحد ، وهو أن الأصل في الكلام : الحقيقة . لا دليل لنا على صحة هذه المقدمة ، إلا أن نقول : لو أراد المتكلم من كلامه غير حقيقته ، مع أنه لم يدل على مراده ولم يبينه ، لزم كونه جاهلاً سامياً في إيقاع التلبيس والتدليس أو نقول : يلزم منه تكليف ما لا يطاق . إلا أنا نقول : من اعتقد أنه لا جهل ولا تلبيس ولا تدليس إلا وهو واقع بإيقاع الله ، وبإرادته . فمع هذا المذهب كيف يمكنه تقرير هذه المقدمة ؟ وأما قوله : يلزم منه تكليف ما لا يطاق، فهذا أيضاً وارد على مذهب الجبرية . لأنه تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر ، ثم يأمره بالإيمان. فكذا ههنا . فثبت : أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات هذه المقدمة على مذهب الجبرية ، ومتى تقيد إثباتها، فقد تعذر الاستدلال بالقرآن والأخبار .
The second aspect in explaining that the statement that the servant is not the creator of his own actions prevents acknowledging that the Qur'an is a proof. The reliance on the Qur'an and all verbal evidences is based on one principle, which is that the default in speech is literal meaning. We have no proof for the validity of this premise except to say: If the speaker intended by his speech other than its literal meaning, while he did not indicate his intent nor clarify it, that would necessitate that he be ignorant or aiming to cause confusion and deception. Or we say: it would necessitate imposing an unbearable obligation. But we say: Whoever believes that there is no ignorance, confusion, or deception except that it occurs by God's causing and His will, then with this doctrine how can he establish this premise? As for his saying: it would necessitate imposing an unbearable obligation, this also applies to the Jabriyya doctrine, because He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever, then commands him to believe. The same applies here. So it is established that there is no way to prove this premise according to the Jabriyya doctrine, and if its proof is prevented, then inference by the Qur'an and reports becomes impossible.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage argues that the Jabriyya (determinist) view, which holds that God creates all human actions including deception, undermines the very basis for using the Qur'an as a proof. The key principle is that speech is presumed to be literal (al-haqiqah). Usually, this principle is justified by noting that if a speaker intends non-literal meaning without indication, he would be ignorant or deceptive, or it would impose an unbearable burden. However, if one believes that all deception is created by God, then these justifications collapse: the speaker could be God who intentionally creates confusion, and the burden is not unbearable because God is the one who both creates the disbelief and commands belief. Thus the determinist cannot establish the literal presumption, and consequently cannot rely on the Qur'an as evidence.

Key Terms
التمسك بالقرآن
الأصل في الكلام
الحقيقة
التلبيس
التدليس
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The literal presumption in speech is universally accepted and can be used by any school of thought.
Correction: The author shows that the Jabriyya determinist view, which attributes all deception to God, cannot accept the standard justifications for the literal presumption, thus they cannot rely on it.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim the Jabriyya cannot prove the literal presumption in speech?
A) Because they deny the existence of literal meanings.
B) Because their belief that God creates all actions, including deception, undermines the argument that non-literal speech without indication implies ignorance or deception.
C) Because they reject the Qur'an as revelation.
D) Because they only use analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The literal presumption, essential for using the Qur'an as proof, cannot be justified if one believes God creates all deception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean for your accountability if you chose to reinterpret it without clear evidence? This lesson warns that without the literal presumption, the Qur'an ceases to be a proof. Are you willing to accept God's words as they appear, or do you rationalize them away?
?
The Jabriyya’s belief that all actions are from God prevented them from even using the Qur'an as evidence. Examine your own heart: Do you ever attribute your sins to God’s decree to avoid responsibility? This lesson shows that such thinking undermines the very basis of revelation. Will you repent and take full ownership of your choices?
?
The author argues that if a speaker intends non-literal meaning without indication, it implies ignorance or deception. Do you truly believe that God, in revealing the Qur'an, is clear and trustworthy? Or do you sometimes suspect hidden meanings that let you avoid difficult commands? Today, commit to taking one verse at its literal meaning and obeying it without excuse.

The Jabriyya View Undermines Qur'anic Reliability
Author argues Jabriyya implies ambiguous Qur'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Jabriyya doctrine logically leads to ambiguity in Qur'anic interpretation, undermining its reliability as divine guidance.

Core Idea
If God wills disbelief, He may also will deceptive speech.

This lesson presents the third argument against the Jabriyya. The author shows that if God creates and wills disbelief, then He must also will the means that lead to it, including ambiguous language. This undermines the Qur'an's reliability as a clear source of guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from God's will to the implication about the Qur'an.
The passage is a single sustained argument.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the steps: God wills disbelief → God wills means to disbelief → deception is a means → God may intend non-literal meanings → Qur'an becomes unreliable.
Each step follows necessarily from the Jabriyya premise.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider the devastating implication: if the Qur'an might mean other than its apparent meaning, it cannot be used as clear evidence for anything.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the Jabriyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في تقرير هذا المطلوب : إن مذهب الجبرية : أنه تعالى هو الذي يخلق الكفر في الكافر ويريده ، وكل من أراد شيئاً ، فإنه أيضاً يريد كل ما أفضى إليه . ومعلوم : أن التلبيس والتدليس وإطلاق اللفظ لإرادة غير معناه مما يفضي إلى حصول الجهل والضلال. وذلك يقتضي على مذهب الجبرية أنه تعالى أراد بهذه الألفاظ غير ظواهرها، ومع هذا الاحتمال كيف يمكن التمسك بالقرآن ؟
The third aspect in establishing this claim: The doctrine of the Jabriyya is that He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever and wills it, and whoever wills something, also wills everything that leads to it. It is known that deception, misrepresentation, and using a word to mean other than its apparent meaning are among things that lead to ignorance and error. This implies, according to the Jabriyya doctrine, that He, exalted, intended by these words other than their apparent meanings. And with this possibility, how can one cling to the Qur'an?
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the Jabriyya. If God creates and wills disbelief, then He must also will the means that lead to it, such as deception and ambiguous speech. Since the Jabriyya hold that God creates all actions, they must admit that God could have intended non-literal meanings in the Qur'an, creating ambiguity. This possibility undermines the certainty required for using the Qur'an as evidence.

Key Terms

يريده

التلبيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the possibility of non-literal meaning is remote and does not affect practical use of the Qur'an.
Correction: The argument shows it is a direct and necessary consequence of the Jabriyya view, making the Qur'an unreliable in principle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Jabriyya doctrine imply about God's intent in the Qur'an?
A) That God always intends literal meanings.
B) That God may have intended non-literal meanings because He wills the means to disbelief, including deception.
C) That the Qur'an is not from God.
D) That only the eternal speech is literal.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya doctrine's implication that God may intend non-literal meanings undermines the Qur'an's authority as clear evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were a Jabri, would you be able to open the Qur'an and take any verse at face value as a clear command from God? How would you live with the constant suspicion that God might have intended something else? Could your faith survive that uncertainty?
?
Consider a time you relied on a verse of the Qur'an for guidance. Now imagine that the possibility raised in this lesson—that God may have intended a different meaning—is true. Would that shake your foundation? What does that tell you about the urgency of holding a correct understanding of divine will?

The Defender's Tu Quoque and the Author's Dilemma
Author presents a defender's response and begins his own dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the defender's counter-argument and the author's reframing of the debate through a dilemma about scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites cannot use scripture without assuming what they aim to prove.

This passage presents both a defender's immediate rebuttal to the Mu'tazilite objections and the author's own shift in strategy. The defender argues that the same objections apply to the Mu'tazilites. Then the author, instead of continuing the tu quoque, observes that the Mu'tazilites themselves rely on scriptural evidence to support human agency, and he poses a key dilemma about the basis of that evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the text is cut off at the end. This is part of a longer argument.
The author's sentence ends with 'if it does' – we must infer the rest.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two sections: first, a defender's response (tu quoque) and second, the author's own answer beginning with an observation and a dilemma.
The defender says 'these objections also apply to you'.
3
Analyze the defender's argument
The defender claims that the Mu'tazilite objections undermine their own position because the 'ten aspects' make scriptural evidence uncertain. Note that this is a tu quoque argument, not a full refutation.
The defender does not solve the problem but points out inconsistency.
4
Examine the author's dilemma
The author notes that Mu'tazilites use scripture to prove human agency, then poses a dilemma: either scriptural evidence depends on prior knowledge of human agency or it does not. This sets up a logical trap.
The passage ends here, so we are left to consider both horns.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
Consider why the author shifts from the defender's tu quoque to a more fundamental epistemological question. This shows deeper theological method.
The author is forcing the Mu'tazilites to clarify the basis of their evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن للمعتقد في الجواب أن يقول : هذه الإلزامات أيضاً واردة عليكم وذلك وإن نفينا الكذب في حق الله تعالى . إلا أن الوجوه العشرة التي ذكرناها قائمة . ومع تلك الوجوه العشرة لا يبقى شيء من الدلائل اللفظية : يقينية . فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الموضع واعلم : أن جوابنا عن مجموع هذه السؤالات أن نقول : رأينا المعتزلة ملأوا كتبهم من الاستدلال بالآيات والأخبار، على أن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه . فنقول : جواز التمسك بالدلائل السمعية . إما أن يكون موقوفاً على العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه، أو لا يكون موقوفاً عليه . فإن كان
Know that the believer (in determinism) may say in response: These objections also apply to you (the Mu'tazilites). And although we deny lying with respect to God, exalted, yet the ten aspects we have mentioned remain. And with those ten aspects, none of the verbal evidences remain certain. So this is the summary of the discussion in this place. Know that our answer to all these questions is to say: We have seen the Mu'tazilites fill their books with inference by verses and reports that the servant is the creator of his own actions. So we say: The permissibility of clinging to scriptural evidences either depends on the knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions, or it does not depend on it. If it does...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents a response from a defender of the determinist position (al-mu'taqid) and then begins the author's own final answer. The defender argues that the same objections (the three aspects) also apply to the Mu'tazilites, who deny lying in God but still face the ten aspects (likely arguments that undermine certainty in scriptural evidence). Then the author states his own approach: he observes that the Mu'tazilites themselves use scriptural evidences to prove human agency. He then poses a dilemma: either the validity of scriptural evidence depends on prior knowledge of human agency or it does not. The text cuts off at 'if it does', leaving the argument incomplete.

Key Terms
المعتقد
الإلزامات
الوجوه العشرة
الدلائل اللفظية
الدلائل السمعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The defender's response is a full refutation of the Mu'tazilite objections.
Correction: The defender's response is a tu quoque argument that points out inconsistency but does not resolve the problem; the author then moves to a deeper epistemological dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the author's purpose in mentioning the Mu'tazilites' use of scriptural evidence?
A) To show they are inconsistent.
B) To set up a dilemma about whether scriptural evidence presupposes knowledge of human agency.
C) To prove that human agency is true.
D) To reject scriptural evidence entirely.

Key Takeaway
The author reframes the debate by shifting from a tu quoque argument to a meta-level dilemma about the epistemic basis of scriptural evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a religious text to support a belief without considering whether your interpretation already assumes what you are trying to prove? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of circular reasoning in your own faith? Commit to one area where you will examine your evidence more honestly.
?
When you discuss qadar with others, do you catch yourself demanding that others accept your proof while ignoring the epistemic weaknesses in your own position? How can you humbly submit to the truth even if it challenges your preferred view?
?
This lesson shows a shift from debating points to questioning the foundation of evidence. Are you willing to have your entire framework questioned? What would it cost you to admit that some of your strongest arguments might be built on shaky ground? Pray for the courage to seek truth above winning arguments.

Refuting the Jabriyya's Use of Quranic Proofs
Two arguments showing the Jabriyya's inability to use Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Jabriyya cannot consistently rely on Quranic proofs without self-refutation.

Core Idea
A doctrine that attributes all deception to God undermines the very proof it tries to use.

The Jabriyya claim that God creates all actions, including disbelief and deception. The author argues that if God alone causes confusion and deceit, then ambiguous Quranic verses could mean something other than their apparent sense—making the Quran an unreliable proof for their position. This lesson explores the first two arguments that expose this inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two separate arguments.
The first argument deals with the premise about ambiguous speech, and the second is called 'the Third Aspect'.
2
Identify the first argument
The author says if someone speaks ambiguously without clarification, it implies ignorance/deception or obligation of the impossible. The Jabriyya believe all that comes from God, so they cannot use this premise.
3
Identify the second argument (Third Aspect)
Since the Jabriyya believe God creates disbelief and wills its means, ambiguous verses could be intended to mislead, making the Quran unreliable.
This is the Third Aspect of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه غير حقيقته، مع أنه لم يدل على مراده ولم يبينه، لزم كونه جاهلاً سامياً في إيقاع التلبيس والتدليس أو نقول: يلزم منه تكليف ما لا يطاق. إلا أنا نقول: من اعتقد أنه لا جهل ولا تلبيس ولا تدليس إلا وهو واقع بإيقاع الله وبإرادته، فمع هذا المذهب كيف يمكنه تقرير هذه المقدمة؟ وأما قوله: يلزم منه تكليف ما لا يطاق، فهذا أيضاً وارد على مذهب الجبرية. لأنه تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر ثم يأمره بالإيمان. فكذا ههنا. فثبت: أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات هذه المقدمة على مذهب الجبرية، ومتى تقيد إثباتها، فقد تعذر الاستدلال بالقرآن والأخبار.

الوجه الثالث في تقرير هذا المطلوب: إن مذهب الجبرية: أنه تعالى هو الذي يخلق الكفر في الكافر ويريده، وكل من أراد شيئاً، فإنه أيضاً يريد كل ما أفضى إليه. ومعلوم: أن التلبيس والتدليس وإطلاق اللفظ لإرادة غير معناه مما يفضي إلى حصول الجهل والضلال. وذلك يقتضي على مذهب الجبرية أنه تعالى أراد بهذه الألفاظ غير ظواهرها، ومع هذا الاحتمال كيف يمكن التمسك بالقرآن؟

واعلم: أن للمعتقد في الجواب أن يقول: هذه الإلزامات أيضاً واردة عليكم، وذلك وإن نفينا الكذب في حق الله تعالى إلا أن الوجوه العشرة التي ذكرناها قائمة. ومع تلك الوجوه العشرة لا يبقى شيء من الدلائل اللفظية يقينية. فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الموضع.

واعلم: أن جوابنا عن مجموع هذه السؤالات أن نقول: رأينا المعتزلة ملأوا كتبهم من الاستدلال بالآيات والأخبار على أن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه. فنقول: جواز التمسك بالدلائل السمعية إما أن يكون موقوفاً على العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه، أو لا يكون موقوفاً عليه. فإن كان الأول كان إثبات كون العبد موجداً بالدلائل السمعية إثباتاً للأصل بالفرع وإنه يوجب الدور. وإن كان الثاني، فحينئذ لا يلزم من القدح في كون العبد موجداً تعذر الاستدلال بالآيات والأحاديث. فإن قالوا: مقصودنا من ذكر الآيات والأحاديث في بيان كون العبد موجداً ليس هو إثبات ذلك في نفس الأمر، بل مقصودنا إلزام الخصم. وذلك لأن المجبرة لما كانوا معترفين بأن القرآن حجة، فإذا ثبت أن القرآن يدل على كون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه، فقد حصل الإلزام. فنقول: فاقبلوا منا مثله. فإن المعتزلة لما سلموا أن القرآن حجة، ثم دل القرآن على أن موجد أفعال العباد هو الله تعالى، فحينئذ يحصل الإلزام والإفحام. فثبت: أن ما ألزموه علينا لازم عليهم.

وبالله التوفيق
His speech is not its meaning, and since he did not indicate his intended meaning nor clarify it, it necessarily follows that he is ignorant and deceptive in causing confusion and deceit, or we say: it entails obligating what cannot be borne. But we say: whoever believes that there is no ignorance nor confusion nor deceit except that it occurs by God's causing and by His will—so with this doctrine, how can he establish this premise? And as for his saying: 'It entails obligating what cannot be borne,' this also applies to the Jabriyya school. Because He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever, then commands him to believe. So likewise here. So it is established that there is no way to prove this premise according to the Jabriyya school, and whenever its proof is constrained, then relying on the Qur'an and traditions becomes impossible.

The Third Aspect in establishing this claim: The doctrine of the Jabriyya is that He, exalted, is the one who creates disbelief in the disbeliever and wills it, and whoever wills something also wills everything that leads to it. And it is known that confusion, deceit, and using a word to mean other than its meaning lead to the occurrence of ignorance and misguidance. And that requires, according to the Jabriyya school, that He, exalted, intended by these words other than their apparent meanings. And with this possibility, how can one adhere to the Qur'an?

Know: The advocate can respond by saying: These implications also apply to you, and even if we deny lying in the case of God, exalted, yet the ten aspects we have mentioned remain valid. And with those ten aspects, none of the verbal proofs remain certain. So this is the summary of the discussion in this place.

Know: Our answer to all these questions is to say: We have seen the Mu'tazila fill their books with argumentation from verses and traditions that the servant is the creator of his own actions. So we say: The permissibility of adhering to scriptural proofs either depends on knowing that the servant is the creator of his own actions, or it does not depend on it. If the first, then proving that the servant is creator by scriptural proofs would be proving the foundation by the branch, and that entails circularity. If the second, then discrediting the fact that the servant is creator does not entail the impossibility of using the verses and hadiths as proofs. If they say: Our purpose in mentioning the verses and hadiths in showing that the servant is creator is not to prove that in reality, but rather our purpose is to refute the opponent. Because the Jabriyya, since they acknowledge that the Qur'an is a proof, when it is established that the Qur'an indicates that the servant is the creator of his own actions, then refutation is achieved. We say: Accept the same from us. For the Mu'tazila, when they acknowledge that the Qur'an is a proof, and then the Qur'an indicates that the creator of the actions of servants is God, exalted, then refutation and confutation are achieved. So it is established: what they imposed on us is binding on them.

And with God is success.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya's (extreme determinists) ability to rely on scriptural proofs. The author first states a premise: if someone speaks ambiguously without clarifying his intent, he is either ignorant or deceptive, or he demands the impossible. The Jabriyya believe all deception is from God, so they cannot claim that God's ambiguous speech implies ignorance or deception—that would attribute evil to God. The author then shows that the Jabriyya's own doctrine leads to the possibility that God intended other than the apparent meanings of the Quran, which undermines their reliance on it. The author also addresses the Mu'tazila who use Quranic verses to prove human free will. He points out a circularity: if the validity of scriptural proofs depends on already knowing that humans create their own actions, then using those proofs to establish that fact is circular. If not, then discrediting human agency does not invalidate the proofs. Finally, he turns the Mu'tazila's own method against them: if they insist that the Quran is a proof and it indicates that God creates actions, then the same logic forces them to accept determinism. Thus, the argument they use to refute the Jabriyya also refutes them.

Key Terms
جبرية
تلبيس
تدليس
تكليف ما لا يطاق
الوجه الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya can use the Qur'an as a proof like everyone else.
Correction: The Jabriyya's own doctrine that all deception is from God undermines the reliability of the Qur'an's apparent meanings, making it an invalid proof for them.

Checkpoint
According to the author's first argument, why can't the Jabriyya use the premise that ambiguous speech implies ignorance or deception?
A) Because they deny that God speaks.
B) Because they believe all deception comes from God, so they cannot attribute ignorance or deception to Him.
C) Because they believe only humans are capable of deception.
D) Because they consider the Qur'an to be unambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya's deterministic doctrine prevents them from consistently using the Qur'an as a proof because it opens the possibility that God intended other than the apparent meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever assume that the apparent meaning is exactly what God intended for you? If a verse seems ambiguous, how does your heart react – with a desire to understand, or with impatience? What does this lesson teach you about the seriousness of attributing deception to God even implicitly?
?
The Jabriyya's belief that God creates disbelief in a person and then commands belief seems absurd to you. But reflect: Are there moments when you blame God for your own shortcomings, thinking 'He willed it so I am not responsible'? How does this lesson call you to take full ownership of your actions without making God the source of your sins?
?
If you were to adopt the deterministic view that everything including misguidance is directly from God, what would happen to your personal relationship with the Quran? Would you still strive to obey its commands with certainty? This lesson shows that such a belief destroys the very foundation of trust in God's word. Do you truly trust that God's speech is clear and sufficient for guidance? If not, what needs to change in your heart?

The Mutual Implication of the Implications
The author responds to the objection that his arguments apply to him as well.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author acknowledges that his own arguments also apply to his opponents but maintains that ten aspects still undermine certainty in scriptural proofs.

Core Idea
Even if the implications apply both ways, the opponent's position is not saved because the ten aspects remain.

After presenting the arguments against the Jabriyya, the author anticipates a response: that the same implications apply to the Ash'ari or Sunni position. He acknowledges this but points out that even if we deny lying in God, the ten previously mentioned aspects still prevent scriptural proofs from being certain. This lesson examines that dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the advocate's response. It is brief but important for the dialectic.
The advocate is likely a Sunni theologian.
2
Understand the mutual implication
The advocate acknowledges that the same implications apply to him but then references 'the ten aspects' that prevent certainty.
The ten aspects were discussed earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه غير حقيقته، مع أنه لم يدل على مراده ولم يبينه، لزم كونه جاهلاً سامياً في إيقاع التلبيس والتدليس أو نقول: يلزم منه تكليف ما لا يطاق. إلا أنا نقول: من اعتقد أنه لا جهل ولا تلبيس ولا تدليس إلا وهو واقع بإيقاع الله وبإرادته، فمع هذا المذهب كيف يمكنه تقرير هذه المقدمة؟ وأما قوله: يلزم منه تكليف ما لا يطاق، فهذا أيضاً وارد على مذهب الجبرية. لأنه تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر ثم يأمره بالإيمان. فكذا ههنا. فثبت: أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات هذه المقدمة على مذهب الجبرية، ومتى تقيد إثباتها، فقد تعذر الاستدلال بالقرآن والأخبار.

الوجه الثالث في تقرير هذا المطلوب: إن مذهب الجبرية: أنه تعالى هو الذي يخلق الكفر في الكافر ويريده، وكل من أراد شيئاً، فإنه أيضاً يريد كل ما أفضى إليه. ومعلوم: أن التلبيس والتدليس وإطلاق اللفظ لإرادة غير معناه مما يفضي إلى حصول الجهل والضلال. وذلك يقتضي على مذهب الجبرية أنه تعالى أراد بهذه الألفاظ غير ظواهرها، ومع هذا الاحتمال كيف يمكن التمسك بالقرآن؟

واعلم: أن للمعتقد في الجواب أن يقول: هذه الإلزامات أيضاً واردة عليكم، وذلك وإن نفينا الكذب في حق الله تعالى إلا أن الوجوه العشرة التي ذكرناها قائمة. ومع تلك الوجوه العشرة لا يبقى شيء من الدلائل اللفظية يقينية. فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الموضع.

واعلم: أن جوابنا عن مجموع هذه السؤالات أن نقول: رأينا المعتزلة ملأوا كتبهم من الاستدلال بالآيات والأخبار على أن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه. فنقول: جواز التمسك بالدلائل السمعية إما أن يكون موقوفاً على العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه، أو لا يكون موقوفاً عليه. فإن كان الأول كان إثبات كون العبد موجداً بالدلائل السمعية إثباتاً للأصل بالفرع وإنه يوجب الدور. وإن كان الثاني، فحينئذ لا يلزم من القدح في كون العبد موجداً تعذر الاستدلال بالآيات والأحاديث. فإن قالوا: مقصودنا من ذكر الآيات والأحاديث في بيان كون العبد موجداً ليس هو إثبات ذلك في نفس الأمر، بل مقصودنا إلزام الخصم. وذلك لأن المجبرة لما كانوا معترفين بأن القرآن حجة، فإذا ثبت أن القرآن يدل على كون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه، فقد حصل الإلزام. فنقول: فاقبلوا منا مثله. فإن المعتزلة لما سلموا أن القرآن حجة، ثم دل القرآن على أن موجد أفعال العباد هو الله تعالى، فحينئذ يحصل الإلزام والإفحام. فثبت: أن ما ألزموه علينا لازم عليهم.

وبالله التوفيق
His speech is not its meaning, and since he did not indicate his intended meaning nor clarify it, it necessarily follows that he is ignorant and deceptive in causing confusion and deceit, or we say: it entails obligating what cannot be borne. But we say: whoever believes that there is no ignorance nor confusion nor deceit except that it occurs by God's causing and by His will—so with this doctrine, how can he establish this premise? And as for his saying: 'It entails obligating what cannot be borne,' this also applies to the Jabriyya school. Because He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever, then commands him to believe. So likewise here. So it is established that there is no way to prove this premise according to the Jabriyya school, and whenever its proof is constrained, then relying on the Qur'an and traditions becomes impossible.

The Third Aspect in establishing this claim: The doctrine of the Jabriyya is that He, exalted, is the one who creates disbelief in the disbeliever and wills it, and whoever wills something also wills everything that leads to it. And it is known that confusion, deceit, and using a word to mean other than its meaning lead to the occurrence of ignorance and misguidance. And that requires, according to the Jabriyya school, that He, exalted, intended by these words other than their apparent meanings. And with this possibility, how can one adhere to the Qur'an?

Know: The advocate can respond by saying: These implications also apply to you, and even if we deny lying in the case of God, exalted, yet the ten aspects we have mentioned remain valid. And with those ten aspects, none of the verbal proofs remain certain. So this is the summary of the discussion in this place.

Know: Our answer to all these questions is to say: We have seen the Mu'tazila fill their books with argumentation from verses and traditions that the servant is the creator of his own actions. So we say: The permissibility of adhering to scriptural proofs either depends on knowing that the servant is the creator of his own actions, or it does not depend on it. If the first, then proving that the servant is creator by scriptural proofs would be proving the foundation by the branch, and that entails circularity. If the second, then discrediting the fact that the servant is creator does not entail the impossibility of using the verses and hadiths as proofs. If they say: Our purpose in mentioning the verses and hadiths in showing that the servant is creator is not to prove that in reality, but rather our purpose is to refute the opponent. Because the Jabriyya, since they acknowledge that the Qur'an is a proof, when it is established that the Qur'an indicates that the servant is the creator of his own actions, then refutation is achieved. We say: Accept the same from us. For the Mu'tazila, when they acknowledge that the Qur'an is a proof, and then the Qur'an indicates that the creator of the actions of servants is God, exalted, then refutation and confutation are achieved. So it is established: what they imposed on us is binding on them.

And with God is success.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya's (extreme determinists) ability to rely on scriptural proofs. The author first states a premise: if someone speaks ambiguously without clarifying his intent, he is either ignorant or deceptive, or he demands the impossible. The Jabriyya believe all deception is from God, so they cannot claim that God's ambiguous speech implies ignorance or deception—that would attribute evil to God. The author then shows that the Jabriyya's own doctrine leads to the possibility that God intended other than the apparent meanings of the Quran, which undermines their reliance on it. The author also addresses the Mu'tazila who use Quranic verses to prove human free will. He points out a circularity: if the validity of scriptural proofs depends on already knowing that humans create their own actions, then using those proofs to establish that fact is circular. If not, then discrediting human agency does not invalidate the proofs. Finally, he turns the Mu'tazila's own method against them: if they insist that the Quran is a proof and it indicates that God creates actions, then the same logic forces them to accept determinism. Thus, the argument they use to refute the Jabriyya also refutes them.

Key Terms
إلزامات
الوجوه العشرة
دلائل لفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author concedes defeat by admitting the implications apply to him.
Correction: The author does not concede; he notes that the opponent might say that, but he then defers to the ten aspects that still prevent certainty.

Checkpoint
What does the advocate mean by 'the ten aspects' in this passage?
A) Ten Quranic verses that prove human free will.
B) Ten reasons why scriptural proofs cannot be certain.
C) Ten arguments against the Mu'tazila.
D) Ten types of deception.

Key Takeaway
Acknowledging mutual implication does not strengthen the opponent's case if the foundational uncertainties remain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone turns your own argument against you, do you cling to pride or do you honestly examine whether your foundational premises are sound? Which of the 'ten aspects' in your own thinking might be shaky, and are you willing to admit it?
?
This passage reveals a theologian who acknowledges mutual implication but holds fast to a deeper foundation. Is there a truth you claim to hold with certainty that you would not abandon even if all your arguments were refuted? If so, what grounds that certainty—is it scripture, reason, or something else?
?
The author says that because of the ten aspects, no verbal proof is certain. Does this unsettle your confidence in the Quran and hadith? How do you personally navigate the tension between recognizing interpretive challenges and maintaining a firm faith in divine revelation?

Circularity and Turning the Tables on the Mu'tazila
The author's final refutation of the Mu'tazila using a dilemma and a reversal of their method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the author exposes circularity in the Mu'tazila's use of scriptural proofs and then turns their own method against them.

Core Idea
If scriptural proofs rely on already knowing human agency, they cannot prove it—and if not, then discrediting agency doesn't hurt scripture.

The author now addresses the Mu'tazila directly. He points out a dilemma: either the validity of scriptural proofs depends on already knowing that humans create their own actions, which would make proving that via scripture circular, or it does not depend on it, in which case attacking human agency does not invalidate scripture. He then goes on to show that if the Mu'tazila accept the Quran as proof and it indicates God creates actions, then they are refuted by their own method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full passage covering the dilemma and the turnaround.
This is the longest and most complex part.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Identify the two horns: either scriptural proof depends on prior knowledge of human agency (circular) or it does not (agency irrelevant).
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's response
They claim they are only using the Quran to refute the opponent, not to prove reality.
4
See the turnaround
The author uses the same logic: the Quran indicates God creates actions, so the Mu'tazila must accept that or reject the Quran.
This refutes them by their own method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya's (extreme determinists) ability to rely on scriptural proofs. The author first states a premise: if someone speaks ambiguously without clarifying his intent, he is either ignorant or deceptive, or he demands the impossible. The Jabriyya believe all deception is from God, so they cannot claim that God's ambiguous speech implies ignorance or deception—that would attribute evil to God. The author then shows that the Jabriyya's own doctrine leads to the possibility that God intended other than the apparent meanings of the Quran, which undermines their reliance on it. He points out a circularity: if the validity of scriptural proofs depends on already knowing that humans create their own actions, then using those proofs to establish that fact is circular. If not, then discrediting human agency does not invalidate the proofs. Finally, he turns the Mu'tazila's own method against them: if they insist that the Quran is a proof and it indicates that God creates actions, then the same logic forces them to accept determinism. Thus, the argument they use to refute the Jabriyya also refutes them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author agrees with the Mu'tazila that humans create their own actions.
Correction: The author does not endorse either side; he merely shows that the Mu'tazila's method leads to their own refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma presented by the author?
A) Scriptural proofs are invalid.
B) If the validity of scriptural proofs depends on already knowing human agency, then using scripture to prove agency is circular.
C) The Jabriyya reject the Quran.
D) The Mu'tazila have no scriptural proofs.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own method of using the Quran to prove human agency can be turned against them to prove determinism, and their attempt to avoid circularity does not save them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a belief, do you rely on premises that you would reject if used against you? This passage shows the Mu'tazila being caught by their own method—what assumptions do you hold that might be turned against you in the same way?
?
The author uses a logical dilemma to force the Mu'tazila to choose between circularity and self-defeat. Have you ever faced a situation where your own argument had no escape? What did you do?
?
If the Qur'an can be used to prove both human agency and divine control (as the passage suggests), which conclusion are you most resistant to? Are you willing to accept the one you dislike if it is logically forced upon you?

Apparent Contradictions in the Quran on Free Will and Determinism
The author presents the objection that the Quran contains verses supporting both jabr and qadr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the Quran seems to support both determinism and free will, and how this objection is used by critics.

Core Idea
The Quran's verses on free will and determinism are used by opponents to argue for either the denial of prophethood or the corruption of the Quran.

In this lesson, we examine a critical objection raised against the Quran: that it contains contradictory verses on determinism (jabr) and free will (qadr). This objection is not merely theological but is used by skeptics to question the Quran's divine origin. We will see how both the Jabriyya and Qadariyya selectively use verses, and the conclusions drawn by those who reject or accept prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and the English translation to understand the objection being presented.
Pay attention to the two groups mentioned at the end.
2
Identify the two groups of critics
Notice that the objection is used by both those who deny Muhammad's prophethood and those who claim the Quran has been altered.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection is considered powerful—it forces each side into forced interpretations, which is unbecoming of a wise speaker.
This sets up the need for a response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث: أن القرآن هل يصير مطعوناً فيه بسبب ما فيه من الآيات الدالة تارة على الجبر وأخرى على القدر؟

قالوا: إن الجبرية تمسكوا بآيات كثيرة قوية الدلالة على الجبر. والقدرية أيضاً تمسكوا بآيات كثيرة قوية الدلالة على القدر. فترى كل واحد من هذين الخصمين، إذا حاول الجواب عن دلائل خصمه، فإنه يحتاج إلى تأويلات مستكرهة ووجوه متعسفة. ولا يليق بالحكيم أن يتكلم بكلام ويريد به تلك المعاني. فعلمنا منهم أن القرآن مشتمل على التناقض.

ثم الذاكرون لهذا السؤال فريقان: منهم من ينكر نبوة محمد - عليه الصلاة والسلام - فيستدل بهذه الشبهة على قوله. ومنهم من يقر بنبوة محمد - عليه الصلاة والسلام - وحينئذ يستدل بهذه الآيات المتعارضة على أن القرآن قد دخله التغيير والتحريف، وأنه ما بقي كما أنزله الله. فإن من المحال أن يقول الحكيم كتاباً يشتمل على هذا الحد من التناقض.
The Third Research: Whether the Quran becomes criticized due to what is in it of verses indicating sometimes jabr (determinism) and sometimes qadr (free will)?

They said: The Jabriyya hold fast to many verses strong in their indication of jabr. The Qadariyya also hold fast to many verses strong in their indication of qadr. So you see each one of these two opponents, when he attempts to answer the proofs of his opponent, he needs forced interpretations and contrived ways. And it is not fitting for a wise person to speak speech and intend those meanings by it. So we learned from them that the Quran contains contradiction.

Then those who mention this question are two groups: Some of them deny the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and use this doubt to support their claim. And some of them affirm the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and then use these conflicting verses to argue that the Quran has undergone change and alteration, and that it has not remained as God revealed it. For it is impossible that a wise person would say a book that contains this degree of contradiction.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage introduces the third research question: whether the Quran is subject to criticism because it contains verses that appear to support both determinism (jabr) and free will (qadr). The author reports an objection: both the Jabriyya and the Qadariyya use strong Quranic verses to support their positions, and each is forced to adopt forced interpretations when refuting the other. This suggests that the Quran itself is contradictory, which is unbecoming of a wise speaker. Two groups use this objection: one denies Muhammad's prophethood based on it, and another affirms his prophethood but claims the Quran has been altered and corrupted, since a wise person would not produce a book with such contradictions.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr/determinism)
قدر (qadr/free will)
تناقض (contradiction)
تأويل (interpretation)
تحريف (alteration/corruption)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection presented, what do the conflicting Quranic verses on jabr and qadr lead some to claim?
A) That the Quran is perfectly consistent.
B) That the Quran has been altered or that Muhammad is not a prophet.
C) That both Jabriyya and Qadariyya are correct.
D) That the Quran should be interpreted literally.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's apparent contradiction on free will and determinism is used by critics to argue for either the denial of prophethood or the corruption of the text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt troubled by Quranic verses that seem to conflict regarding your own responsibility before God? When you encounter a verse that appears to contradict your understanding, do you immediately seek a forced interpretation, or do you trust that the Quran is from God and wait for knowledge? Will you commit to patience and seeking deeper understanding rather than succumbing to doubt?
?
The objectors argued that forced interpretations are unbecoming of a wise speaker. How do you respond when you cannot reconcile two truths taught in the Quran? Are you willing to accept that your limited intellect may not grasp the full harmony of divine speech, or do you become suspicious of the text itself? This lesson challenges you to examine your own heart: are you ready to submit to the Quran even when it perplexes you?

Razi's Rebuttal of the Circularity Objection on Human Agency
Razi rebuts the charge that using scriptural proofs for human agency leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the charge that using scriptural proofs for human agency leads to circularity by showing that the objection is self-defeating.

Core Idea
The opponent's objection applies equally to them—Razi turns the tables.

Razi confronts a classic objection: Isn't using scripture to prove human agency circular? He unpacks the dilemma and then shows that even if the opponent falls back on a polemical strategy, the same tactic can be used against them, thus neutralizing the objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's dilemma
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent presents two possibilities: either proving human agency by scripture is circular, or it is not. Identify the two horns.
The opponent is trying to trap Razi in a logical inconsistency.
2
See Razi's initial move
Notice Razi's response to the second horn: if the proof is not circular, then criticizing the servant as creator does not invalidate using the verses.
This is a subtle but important defensive move.
3
Grasp the opponent's fallback position (ilzam)
The opponent clarifies they are not proving reality but compelling the Jabriyyah by their own acceptance of the Qur'an. Understand the concept of 'ilzam' (compelling the opponent).
This shifts the argument from truth to polemics.
4
Follow Razi's counter-argument
Razi turns the table: the same ilzam method applies to the Mu'tazila because the Qur'an indicates God creates actions. See how he mirrors the opponent's logic.
This is the key move that makes the objection self-defeating.
5
Realize the conclusion
Razi concludes that what the opponent compelled them with is binding upon the opponent themselves. The objection fails.
The argument is about logical consistency, not truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول كان إثبات كون العبد موجداً بالدلائل السمعية ، إثباتا للأصل بالفرع وإنه يوجب الدور . وإن كان الثاني ، فحينئذ لا يلزم من القدح في كون العبد موجداً ، تعذر الاستدلال بالآيات والأحاديث . فإن قالوا : مقصودنا من ذكر الآيات والأحاديث في بيان كون العبد موجداً : ليس هو إثبات ذلك في نفس الأمر ، بل مقصودنا : إلزام الخصم . وذلك لأن المجبرة لما كانوا معترفين بأن القرآن حجة . فإذا ثبت أن القرآن يدل على كون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، فقد حصل الإلزام . فنقول : فاقبلوا منا مثله . فإن المعتزلة لما سلموا أن القرآن حجة ، ثم دل القرآن على أن موجد أفعال العباد هو الله تعالى . فحينئذ يحصل الإلزام والإفحام . فثبت : أن ما ألزموه علينا ، لازم عليهم . وبالله التوفيق
The first: establishing that the servant is a creator (mūjid) by scriptural proofs is establishing the original by the branch, and that leads to circularity (dawr). And if it is the second, then it does not follow from the criticism of the servant being a creator that it is impossible to use verses and hadiths as evidence. For if they say: Our intention in mentioning the verses and hadiths in clarifying that the servant is a creator is not to establish that in reality, rather our intention is to compel the opponent (ilzām). That is because the Jabriyyah, since they acknowledge that the Qur'an is a proof, if it is established that the Qur'an indicates that the servant is a creator of his own actions, then compulsion is achieved. So we say: Accept from us the like of it. For the Mu'tazila, since they acknowledge that the Qur'an is a proof, and then the Qur'an indicates that the creator of the actions of servants is God Almighty, then compulsion and silencing (ifḥām) are achieved. So it is established: what they compelled us with is binding upon them. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against using scriptural proofs for human agency. The opponent presents a dilemma: either proving human agency by scriptural proofs is establishing the original by the branch (leading to circularity), or it is not. Razi argues that if the first, it is circular, but if the second, then criticizing that proof does not invalidate using the verses. Then the opponent says they only use the verses as a polemical argument against the Jabriyyah (who accept the Qur'an's authority), not to establish the reality. Razi responds by saying that the same method can be used against them: since the Mu'tazila accept the Qur'an as proof, and the Qur'an indicates that God is the creator of actions, they are equally compelled. Thus the objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
العبد موجداً
الدلائل السمعية
الدور
الإلزام
المجبرة
المعتزلة
الإفحام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the actual truth of human agency.
Correction: The argument is about the logical consistency of the opponent's position.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's response to the objection about using scriptural proofs for human agency?
A) He concedes that the proofs are circular but still valid.
B) He argues that the objection applies equally to the objector's own method.
C) He denies that the Qur'an can be used as evidence.
D) He states that only reason can decide the issue.

Key Takeaway
Razi's response shows that the objection of circularity based on using scriptural proofs for human agency is self-defeating because the same method is equally binding on the objector.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that his opponents use scripture to compel others, but when the same method is applied to them, they reject it. When have you held others to a standard you were unwilling to apply to yourself? What would it take for you to honestly accept that inconsistency today?
?
The objection of circularity is turned back on the objector. Think of a conviction you hold that might be based on a premise you only accept because it supports your view. Are you willing to examine whether that premise could just as easily support the opposite position?
?
Razi's argument does not settle the truth of human agency but exposes a logical flaw in the opponent's critique. In your own spiritual life, are you more concerned with winning arguments or with submitting to truth, even when it means your own position is challenged? What step can you take this week to prioritize truth over consistency?
